BOYD v. State


No. F-86-679


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK CR 40; 839 P.2d 1363; 1992 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 57; 63 O.B.A.J. 1842



June 18, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 7, 1992. Rehearing Denied November 24, 1992. Released for Publication November 24, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE JAMES L. GULLETT, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted defendant of first degree malice aforethought murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7 (1981) and robbery with firearms in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 801 (1981). The jury found three aggravating circumstances and sentenced defendant to death. Defendant had shot a police officer investigating the robbery of a convenience store.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended on appeal that the trial court erred in failing to sue sponte instruct the jury on the lesser included offenses of first degree manslaughter and second degree murder and that he had ineffective assistance of counsel at trial. The court disagreed and affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that the evidence presented was insufficient to support an instruction on first degree manslaughter or second degree murder, because to have been entitled to such instructions, the evidence had to show that the homicide was perpetrated without a design to effect death by means of a dangerous weapon. The court found that the evidence showed that after defendant shot the officer the first time, he placed the barrel against the officer's chest and fired the weapon a second time and that it was impossible for rational juror to have found that such death was not the result of a premeditated design. As to an alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, the court found that the record was devoid of any evidence to have indicated that defendant's counsel should have been successful on the motion to suppress and that other tactical decisions of the attorney were not inadequate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

Stiles v. State


No. F-86-314


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK CR 23; 829 P.2d 984; 1992 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 33; 63 O.B.A.J. 1064



April 1, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TULSA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE JAY D. DALTON, DISTRICT JUDGE



DISPOSITION: The Judgment and Sentence for First Degree Robbery is REVERSED and REMANDED with instructions to DISMISS. His Judgment and Sentence for Murder in the First Degree is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions of felony murder and robbery with a firearm in the District Court of Tulsa County (Oklahoma), which, as recommended by the jury, sentenced defendant to death by lethal injection for the murder count and 200 years imprisonment for the robbery.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of both robbery and felony murder where the robbery served as the predicate felony for the shooting death of a restaurant employee, who had attempted to thwart the robbery. The jury found the existence of two aggravating circumstances under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12(1) and (7) (1981) and recommended the death penalty. On appeal, the court rejected defendant's charges of prosecutorial misconduct, erroneous jury instructions, and improper eyewitness in-court identification. The court also held that the specific statutory procedure designed for capital cases found in Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.13(E)(2) (Supp. 1985) took precedence over the general statutes of criminal procedure found in Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 936 (1981). Although the court did affirm defendant's conviction for felony murder and the sentence of death imposed under its independent review of capital cases, Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §701.13(C) (Supp. 1985), the court reversed the robbery conviction and sentence. Because the robbery conviction had merged into the felony murder offense, convictions for both offenses violated defendant's right to not twice be put in jeopardy for the same offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's judgment and sentence for first degree robbery and remanded to the district court with instructions to dismiss, but affirmed defendant's conviction for felony murder and his sentence to death.

State v. Blevins


Case No. O-91-302


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK CR 4; 825 P.2d 270; 1992 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 3; 63 O.B.A.J. 539



February 11, 1992, Decided  
February 11, 1992, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] Released for Publication February 11, 1992


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 13, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State filed sought writs of prohibition and/or mandamus against respondent, a judge of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), after the judge refused to sustain the State's motion for discovery in a criminal case on the ground that the Allen rule, a judicially-announced discovery rule, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In a criminal case, the district court judge refused to sustain the State's motion for discovery and ruled that the Allen rule, a judicially-announced criminal discovery rule set forth in a previous decision of the Court of Criminal Appeals, was unconstitutional. The State filed an application for the court to assume original jurisdiction and issue a writ of prohibition and/or mandamus. The criminal case was stayed until the court ruled. Upon review, the court granted the application and issued a writ of prohibition to the district court judge. Under Okla. Const. art. 7, § 4, and Okla. Stat. tit. 20 § 40 (1981), the district court judge was required to follow the opinions and orders of the court until the court found it necessary to overrule those opinions or orders. The court prohibited the district court judge from enforcing his previous order, lifted the stay, and directed the trial of the case to proceed.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of prohibition against the district court judge, prohibiting him from enforcing his previous order denying the State's motion for discovery, lifting the stay, and directing the trial of the criminal case to proceed.

Horner v. State


No. F-90-0052


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK CR 46; 836 P.2d 679; 1992 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 64



August 4, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 29, 1992. Released for Publication September 29, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OSAGE COUNTY. THE HONORABLE J.R. PEARMAN, DISTRICT JUDGE



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction on six counts of the crime of possession of a stolen vehicle by the District Court of Osage County (Oklahoma). He argued that the searches that led to his arrest were illegal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant operated a vehicle salvage yard. Law officers and a staff member from the Oklahoma Used Motor Vehicle & Parts Commission visited defendant's business and conducted a warrantless administrative investigation. Days later police and deputies arrived at the premises with a search warrant, which was obtained based on information gathered during the first search. They found stolen vehicles and automobile parts. Two days later a warrantless search was conducted on some land about two miles away from the salvage yard. The police found stolen vehicles, which were eventually seized. Defendant was arrested, tried, and convicted. He appealed. The court rejected defendant's argument that the initial search was an illegal warrantless search disguised as an administrative investigation. The court rejected his contention that the statute authorizing such investigations was unconstitutional. The court found that the subject search met the three or four criteria used to determine the constitutionality of such searches. Since the first search met constitutional muster defendant's argument that the others were tainted by it was meritless. The search on open land did not require a warrant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.

Newton v. State


No. F-89-927


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


1991 OK CR 127; 824 P.2d 391; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 133; 63 O.B.A.J. 34



December 31, 1991, Filed


NOTICE: Released for Publication February 4, 1992.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 4, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF WASHINGTON COUNTY. THE HONORABLE JOHN G. LANNING, DISTRICT JUDGE



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the District Court of Washington County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of unlawful possession of a controlled dangerous substance with intent to distribute and unlawful possession of drug paraphernalia.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued the affidavit used to obtain the search warrant failed to show probable cause and was based on hearsay of a disclosed informant. The district court convicted defendant. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of Okla. Code Crim. P., Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1089.1 et seq. (1981). On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions holding that the specificity of the details from the informant was sufficient to support a finding the informant had personal knowledge and the fact the affidavit failed to state the informant was a convicted felon was not per se proof of unreliability. Regarding § 1089.1, the court held that while the judge was required to make written findings of fact, the judge here had orally stated his reasons for his decision and gave both defendant and the State notice that he was going to order the preliminary hearing to continue. The court held that even though the findings were not written, the trial judge had fulfilled his obligation to notify the reviewing court of his reasons for ruling as he had. Finally, defendant had failed to show that § 1089.1 was unconstitutional, and statutes were presumed valid unless otherwise shown.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for unlawful possession of a controlled dangerous substance with intent to distribute and unlawful possession of drug paraphernalia.

 

846 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Berget v. State


No. C-87-190


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 121; 824 P.2d 364; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 129; 62 O.B.A.J. 3546



November 13, 1991, Filed


NOTICE: Certified for Publication February 25, 1992.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 25, 1992, Reported at 1992 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 10.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; Before the Honorable John M. Amick, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of first degree murder, burglary in the first degree, and possession of a firearm after former conviction of a felony. He moved to withdraw his plea. The request was denied. He timely filed a petition for writ of certiorari regarding the validity of his plea and accompanying sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and an accomplice forced their victim into his car, drove around, held him at gunpoint, and later shot him. The court held that admitting testimony that defendant gave in the trial of his accomplice was proper. The testimony was not admitted under judicial notice. The evidence was enough to satisfy the requirement that a factual basis for the crime was established. The element of intent was proven through defendant's own statements. Thus, there was no merit to his argument that the record did not establish a factual basis for his plea. Defendant did not make any allegations which would indicate that there was any doubt as to his competency. The trial court did not err in refusing to allow him to withdraw his plea. He never alleged that his plea was involuntary. The decision to allow the withdrawal of a plea was within the sound discretion of the trial court. There was sufficient evidence of torture in the context of the heinous, atrocious, and cruel aggravating circumstances. Based on a thorough reviewing the entire record, the sentence of death was supported by the evidence and it was not imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice or any other arbitrary factor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence.

Burke v. State


No. F-87-975


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 116; 820 P.2d 1344; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 123; 62 O.B.A.J. 3443



November 1, 1991, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] Released for Publication December 9, 1991.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied December 9, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; The Honorable James L. Gullett, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: This case is REVERSED and REMANDED for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the District Court, Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted defendant of first degree rape in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 1111 and 1114 (1984).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his first-degree rape conviction and argued that the videotaped testimony from the child witness was erroneously admitted into evidence under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 752B(1) (Supp. 1986). Defendant asserted that § 752B(1) was unconstitutional because it violated his Sixth Amendment right to face-to-face confrontation. The court determined that the videotaped testimony taken pursuant to § 752B(1) was manufactured hearsay because it was an out of court statement offered to prove the truth of the matter asserted. The fatal flaw of § 752B(1) was that it forever denied defendant the opportunity for cross-examination. The court concluded that § 752B(1) was unconstitutional on its face because it failed to require the witness to take an oath, be subject to cross-examination, and did not allow for the jury to observe the witness to determine her demeanor and credibility.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment and remanded for a new trial.

James v. State

Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 105; 818 P.2d 918; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 114; 62 O.B.A.J. 3008



September 26, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Muskogee County; The Honorable Hardy Summers, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the judgment of the District Court of Muskogee County (Oklahoma), which denied his application for post-conviction relief. He raised as error: improper instruction on aggravating circumstance; failure to give a proper instruction resulted in a sentence modification; sufficiency of the evidence; ineffective assistance of trial counsel; and, denial of a state funded psychiatrist to assist him at trial and at post-conviction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of murder. His conviction and sentence were affirmed on direct appeal and a writ of certiorari with the Supreme Court was denied. The court found that res judicata barred petitioner's assignment of errors for sufficiency of the evidence, ineffective assistance, and denial of a psychiatrist. Those issues were not raised on direct appeal. The court affirmed the judgment, finding that while the improper instruction caused the aggravating circumstance of especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel to fail it did not necessitate an automatic modification of the death sentence. Further the jury found that two other aggravating circumstances existed. The court concluded that reweighing evidence did not violate petitioner's right to have a jury set his sentence because he waived that right under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 701.10(A), (B) (1981). The court found after performing the harmless error analysis to the second stage sentencing errors that the jury was not property instructed, the aggravating circumstance was invalid, but other aggravating circumstances and the mitigating evidence revealed sufficient evidence to support the imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Vanwoundenberg v. State


No. PC-88-825


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 104; 818 P.2d 913; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 115; 62 O.B.A.J. 3011



September 26, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Muskogee County; The Honorable Hardy Summers, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a judgment of the District Court of Muskogee County (Oklahoma) that denied the petitioner's application for post-conviction relief following the petitioner's first-degree murder conviction and death sentence being affirmed. The petitioner sought to reduce his death sentence to a life sentence.


OVERVIEW: The petitioner sought post-conviction relief following an unsuccessful appeal of his murder conviction and death sentence. Post-conviction relief was denied, and the petitioner appealed. The court affirmed, holding that although the jury was not properly instructed as to the aggravating circumstance that the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, the other aggravating circumstances were valid and revealed that sufficient evidence existed to support the imposition of the death penalty. The court had the authority to review the remaining aggravating circumstances to determine the validity of the death sentence, pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.13(C) and 701.13(F) (Supp. 1986). The petitioner was not entitled to have his jury set his sentence, pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 926 (1981), because no such request was ever made. Although Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 701.10(A) and 701.10(B) (Supp. 1989) required a sentencing proceeding before the same jury, the petitioner waived the right and thus, the trial court properly conducted the sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Smith v. State


No. F-87-290


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 100; 819 P.2d 270; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 109; 62 O.B.A.J. 2896



September 20, 1991, Filed


NOTICE: Released for Publication November 19, 1991.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied November 19, 1991.
Writ of certiorari denied Smith v. Oklahoma, 504 U.S. 959, 112 S. Ct. 2312, 119 L. Ed. 2d 232, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 3332 (1992)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Smith v. Gibson, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 46905 (W.D. Okla., May 17, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Canadian County; The Honorable Edward C. Cunningham, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction on charges by the State for murder in the first degree in the District Court of Canadian County (Oklahoma) based on his competency to stand trial, denial of funds for an investigator, improper jury selection and instructions, and evidentiary issues pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 22, §§ 1175, 742 (1981); Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 464(B) (1985); Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701 (1981).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's victim was a passenger in his car, and after he announced his intent to rob the victim to another passenger, he fatally shot the victim. On defendant's pretrial motion, the trial court ordered an examination of defendant by a psychologist, who determined that he was competent to stand trial. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence. Defendant was competent to stand trial under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1175.1(1) (1981) because he could understand the nature of the charges and proceedings against him and was able to assist in his defense. The trial court acted within its discretion under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 464(B) (1985) in the denial of funds for an investigator to assist in the defense because defendant's application for funds was filed prematurely and was not prejudicial to defendant. The court found no error in the jury selection process or the jury instructions. An instruction regarding corroboration of accomplice testimony was not warranted, as the witness was not an accomplice. The court found that evidence in the sentencing stage was relevant to the issue of aggravating circumstances under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12 (1981).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for murder in the first degree by the trial court.

 

851 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Stout v. State


Case Nos. PC-88-1044, PC-90-333


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 98; 817 P.2d 737; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 107; 62 O.B.A.J. 2749



September 11, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Caddo County; Before the Honorable Milburn Jay Adams, District Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a judgment of the District Court of Caddo County (Oklahoma), which convicted petitioner for the first degree murders of his sister and her husband, and sentenced petitioner to death on each count. The jury found two aggravating circumstances. Petitioner also challenged an anti-sympathy instruction given in the second stage of trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner beat his sister and her husband to death. The jury found two aggravating circumstances: (1) that the murders were especially heinous, atrocious, and cruel, and (2) that petitioner created great risk of death to more than one person. On appeal, the court held that the finding of the first aggravating circumstance was invalid because a jury instruction failed to sufficiently channel the sentencer's discretion as required by the U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court found that the remaining aggravating circumstance was that petitioner created great risk of death to more than one person, but during the sentencing stage of trial the prosecutor hardly mentioned the factor at all, focusing instead on heinous, atrocious, or cruel. Thus, the court concluded that it could not determine with certainty that the jury would have recommended the death penalty had it not considered the invalidated aggravator that the murders were heinous, atrocious, or cruel. As to the anti-sympathy instruction, the court held that the instructions properly informed the jury that it was to consider all mitigating evidence and it was not to allow arbitrary sympathy to enter into its sentencing decision.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the district court for resentencing and affirmed the denial of the challenge of the anti-sympathy instruction.

 

852 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


State v. Pratt


No. S-90-724


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 95; 816 P.2d 1149; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 104; 62 O.B.A.J. 2552



August 23, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Tulsa County; The Honorable William Musseman, Special Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State, under a reserved question of law under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1053.1 (Supp. 1981), appealed the judgment of a special judge in the District Court of Tulsa County (Oklahoma), who sustained defendant's demur to an information that charged him with assault and battery on a police officer under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 649 (Supp. 1989). The special judge held that § 649 was unconstitutionally vague and overprotective of a class of citizens.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with assault and battery on a police officer. The court reversed the special judge's order that sustained defendant's demur to the information. The court accepted the State's appeal although the filing was past the 10-day requirement of Okla. Ct. Crim. App. R. 2.5, 2.1(B), Okla. Stat. tit. 22, ch. 18, app. (Supp. 1989). An automatic appeal was mandated, and the State's reliance on an erroneous date in its intent to appeal notice that caused the delay was excusable neglect. The special judge erred in determining that Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 649 (Supp. 1989) was unconstitutionally vague because he misread the statute's language. Section 649(A) related to a misdemeanor offense of assault, and § 649(B) defined the felony offense as battery, or assault and battery. Section 649 was also not violative of equal protection although it protected police officers. Section 649 bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest. The legislature, in providing greater punishment for assault, assault and battery, or battery of a police officer versus other citizens, attempted to provide additional protection to police officers during the performance of their duties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the special judge that was in favor of defendant and remanded the action.

Shipman v. State


No. F-85-321


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 93; 816 P.2d 571; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 102; 62 O.B.A.J. 2542



August 13, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An appeal from the District Court of Muskogee County; The Honorable William H. Bliss, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the District Court of Muskogee County (Oklahoma), which, upon a jury verdict, convicted defendant of one count of lewd molestation. Defendant was assessed punishment at eight years imprisonment. Defendant had been tried for three counts of lewd molestation. Defendant was acquitted of the other two counts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of molesting a three and a half-year-old girl. At trial, several people testified that defendant had molested the alleged victim. However, the testimony at trial was largely confusing and contradictory. The child's mother testified that the child had indicated that someone other than defendant had molested her. Pursuant to Okla. Code Crim. P., Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 753 (1984), the deposition of the child was video taped. The video taped deposition was admitted at trial and shown to the jury. Defendant argued that the showing of the video tape violated his right to be confronted with the witnesses against him as was guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. VI and Okla. Const. art. II, § 20. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that: (1) Okla. Code Crim. P., Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 753 (1984) was constitutional on its face; (2) where there was no hearing to determine whether the video taped testimony was necessary to protect the child, Okla. Code Crim. P., Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 753 (1984) was unconstitutionally applied in this case; and (3) defendant was deprived of his constitutional right to confront the witnesses against him.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction of lewd molestation and remanded the case for a new trial.

State v. Uriarite


No. S-90-1127


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 80; 815 P.2d 193; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 83; 62 O.B.A.J. 2253



July 16, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Washington County; the Honorable Janice P. Dreiling, Associate District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed a ruling entered in the District Court of Washington County (Oklahoma), which led to the dismissal charges against defendant that included unlawful distribution of methamphetamine and unlawful possession of methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for an undercover sale of 1/8th ounce of methamphetamine and possession of 168.8 grams of methamphetamine in his house, which was located within 1000 feet a school. He pleaded guilty to unlawful distribution of methamphetamine within 1000 feet of a school under Okla. Stat. § 2-401A and unlawful possession of a controlled dangerous substance with intent to distribute under Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401A. The court held that Section 2-401 was a lesser-included offense of Section 2-401A. The court also found Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401A(D), which mandated that the sections did not merge, did not pass constitutional muster. Where a single act constituted a violation of both Section 2-401 and Section 2-401A, an application of Section 2-401A(D) would have violated the state and federal double jeopardy clauses. The court determined that all of Section 2-401A was unconstitutional because it had no severance clause. The court found, however, the holding left no gap in the statutes because the same criminal conduct was proscribed by Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401 (Supp. 1989), which required less proof than Section 2-401A and provided a greater potential for punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

Stafford v. State


No. PC-89-87


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 77; 815 P.2d 685; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 88; 62 O.B.A.J. 2261



July 16, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; the Honorable Charles Owens, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought review of a judgment from the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which denied his second application for post conviction relief. Petitioner had been convicted of six counts of murder in the first degree and was sentenced to death on all six counts.


OVERVIEW: In his second application for post conviction relief, petitioner alleged that the jury instruction concerning the "especially heinous, atrocious or cruel" aggravating circumstance was unconstitutionally vague. The district court held that resolution of this issue was barred by the doctrine of res judicata because petitioner could have raised it on direct appeal or in his first application for post conviction relief. On appeal, the court addressed the merits of his contention because a United States Supreme Court decision constituted an intervening change in the law. The court agreed that the aggravating circumstance failed because the jury based its finding of such circumstance upon an unconstitutionally vague instruction. However, it reweighed the remaining aggravating circumstances against the mitigating evidence and affirmed. The reweighing process did not deny petitioner his right to sentencing by a jury because under state law, the court was allowed to reweigh as part of the authorized sentence review. Its authority to reweigh was not limited to review on direct appeal. The court's exercise of its statutory review powers did not constitute an ex post facto violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of petitioner's application for post-conviction relief.

Howard v. State


No. F-90-0304


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 76; 815 P.2d 679; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 81; 62 O.B.A.J. 2246



July 15, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; before the Honorable Bryan C. Dixon, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions and sentences in the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), after a jury trial, for possession of a controlled dangerous substance, in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401 (1981), and for maintaining a place where controlled dangerous substances were kept, in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-404(A)(6) (1981), both of which were after former conviction of two or more felonies.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for public intoxication and asked the police to take him back to his motel room so he could make arrangements for his son. The police found methamphetamine in the motel room. Defendant claimed that the evidence failed to support his convictions, that his former convictions were invalidly introduced, and that his sentences were excessive. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part defendant's convictions. The court held that defendant rented the motel room, that drugs were found in the motel room, that the evidence supported the possession charge, that the State's introduction of a prior reduced charge was harmless error, and that defendant's sentence for possession was not excessive. The court held, however, that the elements for possession and for maintaining a place under § 2-404(A)(6) were distinctly different, that the evidence failed to prove that defendant's motel room was being used for the purpose of facilitating drug usage or drug sales, that there was no proof of any offense other than simple possession, and that defendant's conviction under § 2-404(A)(6) was required to be reversed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of a controlled dangerous substance after former conviction of two or more felonies. The court reversed defendant's conviction for maintaining a place where controlled dangerous substances were kept.

 

857 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


State v. Tinkler


No. S-90-1055


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 73; 815 P.2d 190; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 80; 62 O.B.A.J. 2244



July 12, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Pushmataha County; before the Honorable Lowell R. Burgess, Associate District Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed the judgment of the District Court of Pushmataha County (Oklahoma), which held that the procedures established by the legislature and codified at Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 751 (1989), were unconstitutional after a certified copy of a forensic report was offered at defendant's preliminary examination on charges of drug trafficking.


OVERVIEW: The State failed to indicate under which subsection of Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1053 (1981), the appeal was pursued. The court treated the appeal as arising under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1053.1 (1981), which required the district attorney to appeal all decisions wherein an Oklahoma statute had been declared unconstitutional. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that a certified copy of a forensic report was sufficiently reliable to have been admissible without supporting testimony offered as proof by the State that a crime in fact had been committed. The court found that the preliminary examination was not a trial. The standard of proof at a preliminary hearing was decidedly lower than the "beyond a reasonable doubt" standard which must have been met at trial. In light of that differing burden of proof, the legislature, by enacting Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 751 (1981), decided that for the preliminary examination only, an additional exception to the rule against hearsay evidence was warranted. The court found that the exception had not resulted in the denial of defendant's right to confrontation because the standard of proof at a preliminary hearing was decidedly lower than at trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and confirmed the constitutionality of the applicable statute involving a certified copy of a forensic report offered at defendant's preliminary examination on charges of drug trafficking.

Duvall v. State


No. F-87-392


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 64; 825 P.2d 621; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 70; 62 O.B.A.J. 1791



May 28, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 2, 1992, Reported at 1992 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 11.


PRIOR HISTORY: An appeal from the District Court of Stephens County; the Honorable George W. Lindley, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was tried for the crime of murder in the first degree in the District Court of Stephens County (Oklahoma). The jury returned a verdict of guilty and recommended punishment at death after determining the aggravating circumstances that defendant was previously convicted of a felony involving the use or threat of violence, and that the murder was especially heinous, atrocious or cruel. Defendant was sentenced accordingly and he appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged, inter alia, that the trial court error in admitting evidence of his previous acts of violence in violation of the hearsay rule. In light of defendant's failure to object to the notice requirements at the time and manner prescribed by the law, he had waived his right to claim error based on those grounds. Thus, the decedent's fear of defendant was relevant. Defendant alleged that the trial court erred in failing to instruct on either first-degree manslaughter or second-degree murder. Considering the nature of the wounds and the surrounding circumstances, a rational juror could not find from the evidence that the victim's death was not the result of a premeditated design to effect death. Clearly there was no evidence that the death arose from heat of passion. Therefore, defendant was not entitled to such instructions. There was no evidence that the verdict would have been different had the jury heard that the victim had a prior conviction; such evidence was properly excluded as inadmissible. While certain prosecutorial remarks were unnecessary and were not to be condoned, they were not so improper as to have affected the verdict of the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence with regard to murder in the first degree.

Williamson v. State


No. F-88-501


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 63; 812 P.2d 384; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 67; 62 O.B.A.J. 1674



May 15, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 9, 1991. As Corrected September 9, 1991. Released for Publication September 9, 1991.

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the District Court of Pontotoc County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of murder in the first degree and, upon the jury's recommendation, sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Over four years after the victim was found dead, defendant was charged with her murder. After a jury trial, defendant was convicted and was sentenced to death. In affirming, the court held (1) that the pre-accusation delay in filing the murder charge against defendant did not deny him due process of law since there was good cause for the delay and he failed to establish prejudice by the delay, (2) that substantial independent evidence was presented that directly established both the corpus delicti and the truthfulness of defendant's admissions and confessions, (3) that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting several pieces of photographic evidence since defendant failed to demonstrate that he was prejudiced by the slight repetition of photographs, (4) that the evidence of defendant's prior unadjudicated acts of violence along with the callous nature in which defendant killed the victim supported the jury's finding of the continuing threat aggravating circumstance in recommending the sentence of death, and (5) that defendant failed to show that his sentence was rendered unreliable by a breakdown in the adversary process caused by ineffective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and the sentence of death.

Thomas v. State


No. F-87-802


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 58; 811 P.2d 1337; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 60; 62 O.B.A.J. 1519



May 10, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 29, 1991. Rehearing Denied June 21, 1991. Released for Publication June 21, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Creek County; Before the Honorable G. Bruce Sewell, Associate District Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the District Court of Creek County (Oklahoma) that rendered judgment upon a jury verdict convicting defendant of first degree murder and robbery by force and imposed the death penalty according to the jury's recommendation.


OVERVIEW: The victim was found dead in her home the day after she left a bar with defendant. At the trial, the medical examiner testified that she died as a result of a severe beating, which included manual strangulation, and four stab wounds. On appeal, defendant raised several grounds of error including the admission of certain testimony, evidence, and jury instructions. The court ruled that there were no findings of reversible error. The court held that: (1) the late endorsement of certain witnesses was waived because defendant did not request an immediate continuance; (2) defendant was not prejudiced when inadmissible hearsay was presented to the jury because he failed to cure the error by requesting admonition of the jury; (3) testimony identifying defendant's tattoo was relevant to identify defendant as the man who left the bar with the victim; (4) the probative value of the photographs showing the victim's dead body was not outweighed by the prejudicial effect because the pictures were relevant to establish the corpus delicti, the injuries, and intent; and (5) the verdict instructions were proper because, taken as a whole, they properly advised the jury of the applicable law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and sentence against defendant.

Sellers v. State

PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County, Oklahoma; The Honorable Charles L. Owens, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: The jury's findings of guilt and the sentences of death are AFFIRMED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) convicting him of three charges of murder in the first degree. The jury recommended a sentence of death for each, and the district court entered judgments and sentences accordingly.


OVERVIEW: The evidence showed that before killing a store clerk, defendant showed his friend the gun he had and said that he wanted to see what it felt like to kill somebody. Defendant later bragged of his conduct. Six months later he shot and killed his own parents, taking precautions to make sure that they were dead and to make sure no one had heard the shots. He conceived an elaborate plan to "discover" his parents dead in bed. Affirming, the court held that the repeated incidences of violence and the callused manner of defendant's actions supported a finding of aggravating circumstance in all three homicides. Even though the court found that the murders were not especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12(4), and the jury's finding of this aggravating circumstance had to fail, the court found that the evidence did support the jury's finding of the aggravating circumstance that defendant would constitute a continuing threat to society and that he knowingly created a great risk of death to more than one person when he killed his parents. The weight of these aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances defendant presented.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the jury's findings of guilt and the sentences of death. Finding no error warranting modification, the judgments and sentences of the district court were affirmed.

Armstrong v. State


No. F-88-503


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 34; 811 P.2d 593; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 35; 62 O.B.A.J. 950



March 18, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied June 26, 1991. Released for Publication June 26, 1991. As Corrected July 16, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Pottawatomie County; Before the Honorable Glen Dale Carter, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment and sentence of the District Court of Pottawatomie County (Oklahoma), which convicted defendant after a jury returned a verdict of guilty to conspiracy to violate the Oklahoma Securities Act, selling an unregistered security, fraud in connection with the offer and sale of a security, and acting as an unregistered investment advisor, agent or broker/dealer.


OVERVIEW: Two women invested in companies based on defendant's advice. Neither he nor the securities were registered with the State. Neither woman had experience in investment, and both lost the entire amount they risked. He was tried and convicted, and the trial court ordered that the sentences on each count were to be served consecutively. On appeal, the court affirmed. Defendant waived claims of all but fundamental error when he failed to object to any jury instructions during trial. The trial court instructed the jury on the only exemption defense supported by the evidence. It heard evidence of the alleged conspiracy in camera and determined that there was a conspiracy. The fact that the prosecutor put on hearsay conspiracy evidence in the trial before proof of the conspiracy was not error under the circumstances. The Oklahoma Securities Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 71, § 1 et seq. (1981), was neither vague nor overbroad. Objections to the prosecutor's actions were not preserved for appeal, and the court would not consider an argument other than the one made at trial. The consecutive sentences were not injected with passion and prejudice and did not shock the conscience of the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence of the trial court.

Williams v. State


No. F-87-700


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 28; 807 P.2d 271; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 29; 62 O.B.A.J. 814



March 1, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; The Honorable Jack R. Parr, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: The convictions for First Degree Murder and First Degree Robbery are AFFIRMED. The conviction for First Degree Burglary is REVERSED and REMANDED with instructions to DISMISS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the judgment denying a request made by defendant, a 16-year-old, that sought to certify her as a juvenile was affirmed, defendant appealed the judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) that convicted defendant of first-degree murder, a violation Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 701.7 (Supp. 1972), first-degree burglary, a violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1431 (1981), and robbery, a violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 791 (1981).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged as an adult for the crimes of murder, burglary, and robbery. Her motion to be certified as a juvenile was denied and defendant was convicted of the charged crimes. After the denial of juvenile certification was denied, defendant appealed her convictions. The court affirmed the murder and robbery convictions. The court reversed the burglary conviction. Because the jury was given alternative murder theories and failed to specify which theory was relied upon in convicting defendant of murder, the court concluded that defendant was convicted under the felony-murder theory and reversed the burglary conviction. The trial court did not err in failing to instruct the jury on the lesser-included offense of second-degree murder, as no rational trier of fact could have found that the death was the result of anything but a premeditated design to effect death. The admission of photographs of the victim's body was not an abuse of discretion. Because murder was one of the offense enumerated in Okla. Stat. tit. 10, § 1104.2 (Supp. 1985), police officers were not required to comply with Okla. Stat. tit. 10, § 1109 (1985) when questioning a juvenile.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions for first-degree murder and first-degree robbery. The court reversed the conviction for first-degree burglary, and remanded the cause with instructions to dismiss.

Hale v. State


No. PC-89-942


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 27; 807 P.2d 264; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 27; 62 O.B.A.J. 701



February 26, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Pottawatomie; The Honorable Gary Brown, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed an order from the District Court of Pottawatomie (Oklahoma), which denied his application for post-conviction relief. A direct appeal of petitioner's conviction was previously filed with the court and the conviction and death sentence were affirmed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was tried for kidnapping and murder and he was sentenced to death. He raised 13 allegations of error in his application for post-conviction relief, 11 of which were either raised on direct appeal or could have been raised on direct appeal. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of his application. The court noted that petitioner was barred from asserting any claims which could have been raised in his direct appeal by Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1086 (1981). The court found that the doctrine of res judicata barred consideration in post-conviction proceedings of issues which had been, or which could have been, raised on direct appeal. The court rejected petitioner's contention that he was entitled to a new trial on the basis of newly discovered evidence in the form of a man's statement that petitioner was not the only person involved in the murder. The court determined that the trial court's denial of the motion for a new trial was not an abuse of discretion. The court decided that petitioner did not present sufficient reasons for not seeking allegedly exculpatory evidence from the FBI sooner than he did and decided that he did not fall within an exception to § 1086.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

 

867 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Nuckols v. State


Case No. PC-89-271


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 10; 805 P.2d 672; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 7; 62 O.B.A.J. 456



January 24, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Pottawatomie County; Before the Honorable Glen Dale Carter, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the court denied a prisoner's first motion for post-conviction relief from his murder conviction and death sentence, the prisoner petitioned for habeas corpus relief in federal court. The case was remanded. He filed a second motion for post-conviction relief alleging that the jury instructions on aggravating circumstances were improper. He appealed after the District Court, Pottawatomie County, Oklahoma, denied his second motion.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner argued that the jury was improperly instructed regarding the aggravating circumstance "that the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel" (HAC). The court recognized that its review of the prisoner's first post-conviction relief motion was improperly based on a broader application of HAC. HAC was properly directed to those crimes where the death of the victim was preceded by torture of the victim or serious physical abuse. This required the sentencer to conduct a two-step analysis. The record showed that the prisoner and a friend went hunting for a person to kill and found such a victim. Defendant hit the victim in the head with a hammer. The victim attempted to sit up. Defendant hit him again repeatedly, then stomped and kicked him until he died. The evidence supported a finding that the victim suffered great physical pain before his death. Thus, the death penalty was appropriately considered. The facts also supported an HAC finding on two independent grounds. The murder was shockingly pitiless, and the victim suffered before his death. The jury was not improperly instructed in regards to the "anti-sympathy" instruction. There was no reversible error.


OUTCOME: The decision denying petitioner's second application for post-conviction relief was affirmed.

Neahring v. State


No. M-90-715


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 5; 804 P.2d 1142; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 5; 62 O.B.A.J. 244



January 11, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Payne County; the Honorable Lois Belden, Special Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the District Court of Payne County (Oklahoma), which denied defendant's request for a jury trial with respect to her arrest for running a stop sign and which convicted her of the offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrest for running a stop sign and demanded a jury trial. The special judge denied her request on the ground that the only punishment associated with her offense was a fine and costs. During the trial, defendant did not cross examine the State's witness or present any evidence on her behalf. Defendant was convicted and assessed a fine and costs. She challenged the judgment. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded for a jury trial. The court held that defendant was entitled to a jury trial under Okla. Const. art. 2, § 19 and Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 601 (1981). Further, the court held that the trial court unduly deprived defendant of her constitutional and statutory right to a trial by jury. The court reasoned that for the offense charged, defendant was entitled to a jury because the range of punishment as provided for in Okla. Stat. tit. 47, § 17-101(b) (1981) exceeded a possible fine of $ 20 and provided the possibility for jail time. The pretrial decision of the trial judge constituted a waiver of the defendant's right to a jury without her consent and therefore constituted a deprivation of a fundamental and constitutional right of defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded for a jury trial.

Boltz v. State


No. F-84-765


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1991 OK CR 1; 806 P.2d 1117; 1991 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 2; 62 O.B.A.J. 151



January 7, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied February 13, 1991. Released for Publication February 13, 1991.
Writ of certiorari denied Boltz v. Oklahoma, 502 U.S. 846, 112 S. Ct. 143, 116 L. Ed. 2d 109, 1991 U.S. LEXIS 4486 (1991)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Boltz v. Mullin, 415 F.3d 1215, 2005 U.S. App. LEXIS 15370 (10th Cir. Okla., 2005)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by Boltz v. Jones, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 35534 (W.D. Okla., June 1, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Pottawatomie County, Oklahoma; The Honorable Glenn Dale Carter, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction, after a jury trial, from the District Court of Pottawatomie County (Oklahoma) for murder in the first degree and his sentence to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant stabbed the victim after the victim came to defendant's house. Affirming the death sentence, the court said that the sentence was not imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor. Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.13 (C)(1) (Supp. 1985). Affirming the conviction, the court found sufficient evidence from which the jury could have found that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances. The court found no abuse of discretion after the trial court allowed the State to use the preliminary hearing testimony of a hospitalized witness. The evidence did not support a heat of passion manslaughter instruction. Circumstantial evidence was strong enough for the jury to find that defendant committed a subsequent burglary of the victim's house, and that the evidence made more probable that defendant intended to kill the victim's wife, supporting the aggravating circumstance of continuing threat. The trial court's instructions on evidence of mitigating circumstances were sufficient. The evidence presented to the jury amply supported the finding that defendant was competent to stand trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and the sentence.

Arnold v. State

1990 OK CR 78; 803 P.2d 1145; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 79; 61 O.B.A.J. 3269

DISPOSITION: Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the District Court of Wagoner County (Oklahoma), which convicted defendant of murder in the first degree, Okla. Stat. art. 21, § 701.7 (1981)


OVERVIEW: Defendant's former step-daughter was the ex-girlfriend of the victim. The victim had been repeatedly harassing the step-daughter and had frequently become violent. The victim disappeared after he had refused to leave the step-daughter alone after she gave him her car on the condition that he leave and not come back. The court additionally held that there was no delay in arraignment where defense counsel was the reason for the delay. The court concluded that the recording of defendant's conversations to a third party were properly admissible because defendant had no expectation of privacy in communications to other people who might betray his trust.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

Washington v. State


No. F-84-727


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 75; 800 P.2d 252; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 73; 61 O.B.A.J. 2977



October 29, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) that denied his request for an evidentiary hearing to determine whether he could have met his burden of proof to require the State to provide him with access to a psychiatrist.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of first degree murder and rape. Prior to trial, defense counsel requested that the trial court appoint several experts to aid him in presenting his defense. He sought a psychiatrist to evaluate the possibility that appellant did not know right from wrong and to show mental or emotional disturbance as a mitigating circumstance. The district judge granted appellant's request for these experts, but later determined that he did not have a duty to appoint the requested experts and denied the motion. Since the time of this hearing, the United States Supreme Court has declared otherwise. Defense counsel did not articulate with specificity why he suspected that appellant's sanity was likely to be a significant factor in his defense. However, he did raise enough doubt about appellant's mental state to warrant an evidentiary hearing. Therefore, the court granted the request for an evidentiary hearing to afford appellant the opportunity to present what he could have shown the district court with regard to whether the forensic odontologist and the chemist were required as "basic tools" of his defense.


OUTCOME: The court granted appellant's request for an evidentiary hearing to determine whether appellant could have, at the motion hearing, made the necessary showing of need for the experts he requested.

873 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Kinsey v. State


No. F-86-300


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 64; 798 P.2d 630; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 63; 61 O.B.A.J. 2389



September 17, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 24, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; The Honorable James B. Blevins, District Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted him, upon a jury verdict, of receiving stolen property, a violation of Okla. Stat. art. 21, § 1713 (Supp.1984).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for receiving stolen property in violation of § 1713. The court affirmed his conviction, finding that the jury instructions did not include an unconstitutional statutory presumption that defendant knew the property in his possession was stolen. The instructions required the State to prove that defendant had reasonable cause to believe the property in his possession was stolen, which was proper because there was no requirement that the State prove actual knowledge. The instructions, when read together, advised the jury of the presumption of innocence and required the jury to consider, along with other elements of the crime, whether defendant should have believed that the goods were stolen. The defendant was not entitled to an instruction on his proposed defense that he did not know the goods were stolen because such a position was not actually a defense, because the State had to prove that element of the offense. The court concluded that while defendant was entitled to a jury instruction on his proposed defense provided it was defense and there was evidence in support of it, but the court found no defense supported by the required evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for receiving stolen property.

Buis v. State


No. M-87-707


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 28; 792 P.2d 427; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 25



May 14, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Tulsa County, Oklahoma; The Honorable Gordon D. McAllister, Jr., Special Judge. William Craig Buis, Appellant, was tried by jury for the crimes of State Taxes Due, Operating a Motor Vehicle Without Carrying a Security Verification Form and Driving Under Suspension in Case Nos. TR-87-3583, TR-87-3584 and TR-87-3585, respectfully, in the District Court of Tulsa County. Appellant represented himself pro se. The jury returned verdicts of guilty and set punishment at $ 100.00, $ 250.00, and $ 200.00. The trial court sentenced Appellant in accordance with the jury's verdicts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the District Court of Tulsa County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of three misdemeanor offenses based on an unverified Uniform Violations Complaint.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of state taxes due, operating a motor vehicle without carrying a security verification form, and driving under suspension in case. Defendant was ordered to pay three fines. On review, the court found that defendant received an unverified Uniform Violations Complaint, which served as the information charging him with the crimes. Furthermore, the court found that because the complaint was not verified the trial court had no subject matter jurisdiction. The court found that the defect was fatal because Okla. Const. art. II, § 17 required that nobody could be prosecuted for either a felony or misdemeanor offense without first receiving a duly verified complaint. Similarly, Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 303 (1981) provided that a criminal information was not valid unless verified. Moreover, the court found that subject matter jurisdiction could not be waived. Therefore, the trial court's decision was reversed, and the case was remanded without a bar to further action.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision that convicted defendant of three misdemeanor offenses was reversed and remanded without a bar to further action.

Virgin v. State


No. F-87-790


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 27; 792 P.2d 1186; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 26



May 11, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County; The Honorable James L. Gullett, District Judge. HERBERT A. VIRGIN, appellant, was convicted in the District Court of Oklahoma County, Case No. CRF-87-2O1, of Forcible Oral Sodomy (Count I), Lewd or Indecent Acts with a Child Under the Age of Sixteen (Count II), and Forcible Anal Sodomy (Count III), each After Former Conviction of Two or More Felonies. He was sentenced to imprisonment for one hundred fifty (150) years on each count, with Counts I and II ordered to be served concurrently and Count III ordered to be served consecutively. From these judgments and sentences, appellant appeals to this Court.



DISPOSITION: Appellant's conviction for Forcible Anal Sodomy (Count III) is REVERSED and REMANDED with instructions to DISMISS. The judgments and sentences for conviction of Forcible Oral Sodomy (Count I) and Lewd or Indecent Acts with a Child Under the Age of Sixteen (Count II) are hereby AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed judgments and sentences from the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of forcible oral sodomy, lewd or indecent acts with a child under the age of 16, and forcible anal sodomy, and sentenced him, after former convictions of two or more felonies, to imprisonment for 150 years on each count, the first two counts concurrently and the third count consecutively.


OVERVIEW: A mother and her two daughters moved in with the mother's cousin, who's fiance was defendant. After a few months, one of the daughters told their mother that defendant had shown her magazines that contained pictures of nude people. The mother and the daughters moved out, at which time the daughter revealed that defendant had molested her. At trial, the daughter testified that defendant had molested her on at least five separate occasions. Defendant asserted that the statutes for forcible anal sodomy, Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 886 (1981), 888 (Supp. 1982), were unconstitutionally vague as applied. Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 887 (1981) provided that "sexual penetration" was required to sustain a conviction for such crime. Although there was not doubt that the crime committed was detestable and abominable, the court had to rule that the act of inserting one's finger into another's rectum did not constitute "sexual penetration."


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for forcible anal sodomy and remanded with instructions to the district court to dismiss. The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences for forcible oral sodomy and lewd or indecent acts with a child under the age of 16.

 

877 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Callaway v. City of Edmond


No. M-89-83


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 25; 791 P.2d 104; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 22



April 24, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 31, 1990. Rehearing Denied May 31, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Oklahoma County, Oklahoma The Honorable Karl Gray, District Judge. Ed Callaway, appellant, was convicted in the District Court of Oklahoma County, for violation of Chapter 4.32.010 of the Edmond Municipal Code, (IV Counts), which prohibits the admission of persons under eighteen years of age to enter any pool hall, snooker parlor, billiard parlor, or similar place of business. He was fined $ 70.00 for each count, with all fines and costs suspended. REVERSED and REMANDED with instructions to DISMISS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of violating Edmond, Okla., Municipal Code ch. 4.32.010, which prohibited the admission of persons under 18 years of age to any pool hall, snooker parlor, billiard parlor, or similar place of business. Defendant was fined $ 70.00 for each count, with all fines and costs suspended.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the municipality's ordinance violated the right of persons under the age of 18 to freedom of association. In disagreeing, the court found that persons under the age of 18 did not have a generalized right of "social association" at defendant's place of business. Persons under 18 were not involved in an activity at defendant's business that qualified as a form of intimate association or expressive association protected by the federal constitution. The court found that the purpose of the ordinance was to prohibit gambling, which was a proper public purpose. However, the ordinance was not rationally related to that objective. Singling out pool halls from other amusement establishments was an act of discrimination, not policy. Indeed, the record did not indicate that gambling was ever observed at defendant's business. In the court's view, the mere presence of persons under age 18 could not be equated with illegal conduct. Thus, the ordinance was too broad and could not be justified under Okla. Const. art. 2, § 7 because it did not bear some rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the matter with instructions for the trial court to dismiss the charges against defendant.

 

878 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Barnes v. State


Case No. 0-90-0102


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 23; 791 P.2d 101; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 21



April 20, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner applied for a writ of mandamus against respondent State seeking to prohibit the State from applying amendments to the Oklahoma Prison Overcrowding Emergency Powers Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 57, § 570 et seq. (1989 Supp.) to his sentence.


OVERVIEW: At the time the prisoner pled guilty to operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor § 574 (1984 Supp.) stated that when the prison population reached 95 percent of capacity and the governor declared a state of emergency, inmates meeting certain criteria would be granted time credit of 120 days for every 60 days served, to a maximum of 720 days per year. The prisoner was sentenced to four years. The Act was amended to provide that only 60 days of credit would be given for every 60 days served with a maximum credit of 360 days per year. Okla. Stat. tit. 57, § 574 (1989 Supp). The prisoner argued that the law in effect when he entered his plea became a basis for his plea agreement and that the application of the 1989 amendment was an improper imposition of an ex post facto law. The court held that two conditions must be satisfied for a law to be ex post facto: it must be retrospective and it must disadvantage the offender. The court held that because § 574 was triggered exclusively by the size of the prison population, it could be imposed only on the basis of events which occurred after it was enacted. The court held that it was not an ex post facto law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the prisoner's application for writ of mandamus.

K.L.J. v. State


No. J-90-26


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


1990 OK CR 22; 824 P.2d 361; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 85; 63 O.B.A.J. 123



 April 18, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication January 10, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE RUSSELL D. HALL, SPECIAL JUDGE



DISPOSITION: AFFIRM.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which denied his motion to be certified and tried as a child pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 10, § 1104.2 (Supp. 1989). Defendant was 16 years old at the time that he was charged with distributing cocaine, in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401 (Supp. 1986), after he sold the cocaine to an undercover officer.


OVERVIEW: A magistrate found sufficient evidence to bind defendant over for trial. Neither defendant nor the State found fault with that determination. Defendant had no prior convictions or charges on his record, although he was arrested on one occasion. Defendant's psychological evaluation showed that he had an IQ of 72, and that he admitted to experimentation with alcohol and the weekly use of marijuana. The doctor stated that the jail time had opened a window of "amenability to traditional interventions," and that defendant had a good prognosis if placed in a secure residential facility followed by placement in a post institutional group home. The trial court declined to certify defendant as a child because the Oklahoma Department of Human Services had a greater demand than they had facilities. The Department would not guarantee that defendant would receive the treatment in the juvenile system. The court held that amendments to Okla. Stat. tit. 10, § 1104.2 (Supp. 1989) had not rendered the statute unconstitutional. Further, the trial court did not abuse its discretion because the protection and safety of the public prevailed and doubts were to be resolved against the youth.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Ekstrand v. State


No. H-89-1275


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 21; 791 P.2d 92; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 48



April 4, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed a petition for the Court to assume jurisdiction and issue a writ of habeas corpus to compel immediate release of the prisoner by the respondents, the State and the Oklahoma Department of Corrections, because Okla. Stat. tit. 57, § 138 (Supp.1988) was allegedly an ex post facto law as applied to him in that it lengthened the prisoner's sentence.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus for the court to assume jurisdiction and to order the State and the Department to immediately release the prisoner. The prisoner argued that Okla. Stat. tit. 57, § 138 (Supp.1988) was an ex post facto law as applied to him in that it computed earned time credits in such a way as to allow the prisoner fewer credits than he was given under the previous statute, thereby lengthening the prisoner's sentence. The court directed a response from the State and the Department. The State and Department filed their response. The court held that the prisoner and other similarly situated inmates who were disadvantaged by the amended statute were entitled to the credits allotted under the statute effective on the date their crime was committed, that the prisoner had not shown that he was entitled to immediate release, and that the proper procedure for the prisoner to follow was to file a petition for writ of habeas corpus in the district court of the county where he was restrained. The bare statements by the prisoner were not sufficient evidence for the court to grant the prisoner his requested writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court denied the prisoner's petition for a writ of habeas corpus to compel immediate release of the prisoner by the State and the Department.

State v. Hunter


Nos. S-87-995, S-88-457


Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma


1990 OK CR 13; 787 P.2d 864; 1990 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 11



February 26, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the District Courts of Tulsa and Washington County, The Honorable Clifford Hopper, District Judge and The Honorable Janice Dreiling, Associate District Judge. The State of Oklahoma, Appellant, pursuant to the automatic appeal provisions of 22 O.S. 1981, § 1053.1, appeals two district court rulings in Tulsa County District Court, Case No. CRF-87-3490, and Washington County District Court, Case No. CRF-87-334, declaring the Delayed Sentencing Program for Young Adults, 22 O.S.Supp. 1987, §§ 996-996.3, unconstitutional.



DISPOSITION:  REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, appellant State sought review from an order of the District Courts of Tulsa and Washington County (Oklahoma), which declared the Delayed Sentencing Program for Young Adults, Okla. Stat. tit. 22, §§ 996-996.3 (Supp. 1987), unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The State appealed two trial courts' decisions that declared the Delayed Sentencing Program for Young Adults (DSPYA), Okla. Stat. tit. 22, §§ 996-996.3, unconstitutional, finding that it violated the constitutional doctrine of separation of powers. On appeal, the court held that the DSPYA plan was in lieu of the presentence investigation required by Okla. Stat. tit. 22, §982 (Supp. 1982) and the requirements of both statutes were substantially the same. The court found that to find one unconstitutional would ipso facto declare the other void. The statute required the Department of Corrections to act pursuant to the directions of the sentencing court and the court determined that it did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers. The court held that the DSPYA neither defined crimes nor fixed punishment for failure to adhere to some requirements, but rather provided a procedure, which was invoked after guilt was established, to assess the offender and provide the sentencing court with data upon which the sentence would be based. The court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the orders from the trial courts.
